  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_3d43339b-58f8-4e8c-9b78-e1ad9d93c4b5'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }

      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer8482004879159697448">"منذ أن غادرت لبنان للاستقرار في فرنسا، كم من مرة سألني البعض عن طيب نية إن كنت أشعر بنفسي "فرنسياً" أم "لبنانياً". وكنت أجيب سائلي على الدوام: "هذا وذاك!"، لا حرصاً مني على التوازن والعدل بل لأنني سأكون كاذباً لو قلت غير ذلك. فما يحدد كياني وليس كيان شخص آخر هو أنني أقف على مفترق بين بلدين، ولغتين أو ثلاث لغات، ومجموعة من التقاليد الثقافية. وهذا بالضبط ما يحدد هويتي...". </span>
  <span id="freeText8482004879159697448" style="display:none">"منذ أن غادرت لبنان للاستقرار في فرنسا، كم من مرة سألني البعض عن طيب نية إن كنت أشعر بنفسي "فرنسياً" أم "لبنانياً". وكنت أجيب سائلي على الدوام: "هذا وذاك!"، لا حرصاً مني على التوازن والعدل بل لأنني سأكون كاذباً لو قلت غير ذلك. فما يحدد كياني وليس كيان شخص آخر هو أنني أقف على مفترق بين بلدين، ولغتين أو ثلاث لغات، ومجموعة من التقاليد الثقافية. وهذا بالضبط ما يحدد هويتي...". <br /><br />يتساءل أمين معلوف، انطلاقاً من سؤال عادي غالباً ما طرحه عليه البعض، عن الهوية، والأهواء التي تثيرها، وانحرافاتها القاتلة. لماذا يبدو من الصعب جداً على المرء الاضطلاع بجميع انتماءاته وبحرية تامة؟ لماذا يجب أن يترافق تأكيد الذات، في أواخر هذا القرن، مع إلغاء الآخرين في أغلب الأحيان، هل تكون مجتمعاتنا عرضة إلى الأبد للتوتر وتصاعد العنف، فقط لأن البشر الذين يعيشون فيها لا يعتنقون الديانة نفسها، ولا يملكون لون البشرة عينه، ولا ينتمون إلى الثقافة الأصلية ذاتها، هل هو قانون الطبيعة أم قانون التاريخ الذي يحكم على البشر بالتناحر باسم هويتهم؟ <br /><br />لقد قرّر المؤلف كتابة "الهويات القاتلة" لأنه يرفض هذا القدر المحتوم، وهذا الكتاب يزخر بالحكمة والتبصر والقلق، وكذلك بالأمل.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8482004879159697448" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

<div id="review_1007347198" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1007347198" />
    <a title="Dolors" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18383778-dolors"><img alt="Dolors" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1502350013p2/18383778.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1007347198?book_show_action=true">Jul 26, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dolors" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dolors" href="/user/show/18383778-dolors">Dolors</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780142002575" href="/book/show/212932.In_the_Name_of_Identity">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Everybody, particularly those walking in the opposite direction</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18383778-dolors?shelf=read-before-2011">read-before-2011</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18383778-dolors?shelf=read-in-2014">read-in-2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18383778-dolors?shelf=best-ever">best-ever</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1007347198" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9745436520362797823">The recent political upheaval in my country where a referendum for the independence of Catalonia is to be celebrated presumably in November almost in parallel to the Scottish one prompted me to unbury my copy of this essay which I read many years ago and whose direct prose has often been misjudged as overly simplistic. <br/>Born in Lebanon within a family of Arabic tradition but of Christian faith, forced to exile from his natal land to France in the 70s, the journalist, essayist and writer of histor</span>
  <span id="freeText9745436520362797823" style="display:none">The recent political upheaval in my country where a referendum for the independence of Catalonia is to be celebrated presumably in November almost in parallel to the Scottish one prompted me to unbury my copy of this essay which I read many years ago and whose direct prose has often been misjudged as overly simplistic. <br />Born in Lebanon within a family of Arabic tradition but of Christian faith, forced to exile from his natal land to France in the 70s, the journalist, essayist and writer of historical fiction <i>Amin Maalouf</i> is a man who has learnt to live at sustained crossroads. Straddling the East and the West, the author of this slim but substantial essay published in 1999 ponders the pieces that compound the puzzle of identity and rebels against the madness that incites mankind to kill each other in the name of nation, language or religion. <br />Taking as a starting point his personal experience and focusing on the islamic conflict, Maalouf stirs up the murky waters of the elusive concept of individuality, the inborn sense of belonging, the instict for self-preservation, the aversion to change and the human tendency to assert one's beliefs in terms of unrelenting negation of others. <br /><br /><i>"What makes me myself rather than anyone else is the very fact that I am poised between two countries, two or three languages, and several cultural traditions. It is precisely this that defines my identity. Would I exist more authentically if I cut off a part of myself?"</i> <br /><br />Even though the historical frame that serves to delineate the author's concise analysis of such abstract themes might appear outdated --he addresses warfare zones like Afghanistan, Rwanda, Burundi, the old Yugoslavia and the inherent danger of the resurgence of segregationist ideologies in European countries like Great Britain, Belgium or Spain-- his conclusions cut across the threshold of time and acquire a premonitory quality that confirms the validity of his reflexions.<br /><br />The central idea of Maalouf's essay revolves around identity not coming from a single wellspring but from multiple ones that bear the personal circumstances and unrepeatable historical contexts that shape each one of us into a unique and unparalleled individual. One needs to pay respect to all the paths that converge into the process of creating one's identity, from one's ancestral heritage to religious or political views and preserve this rich diversity. <br />But Maalouf’s initially tolerant approach turns into a passionate voice of protest when he firmly denounces the inherent perversity of obsessive delimitation regarding origin, belief or what is perceived as linguistic repression that can easily drift towards biased judgmentalism and indiscriminate prejudice that perpetrate human conflict giving way to mass killing or genocides. <br /><br />To Maalouf, being human means, above any other distinctive trait, being the carrier of undisputable human rights that prevail over any other component of identity and can’t be overlooked neither because of tradition, ideology, religion or any other piece that compounds the jagged puzzle of identity. With all its multiplicity, there’s only ONE humanity.<br />In order to agree on such basic and overused principle, human rights have to be <i>universal</i> and concepts such as equality, freedom, access to knowledge or to a honorable or dignified life can’t be denied in order to safeguard any religious, ancestral or traditional practice or to cling to an idealized past.<br />In the current context of globalization, the term <i>universality</i> can be dangerously confused with <i>uniformity</i> triggering determinism, resignation or victimhood in individuals whose feelings of alienation and marginalization fester in their distorted image of a multifarious reality.<br /><br />Looking back at the history of mankind, Maalouf is convinced there is a promising future ahead where human dignity, plural fraternity and diversity will co-exist overshadowing hatred, violence and racial segregation without stepping into the false conception of understanding modernity as a conduit to tame every individual’s special identity into an undistinguishable herd of narcotized clones. <br />Aren’t we all migrants, members of minorities and the result of mixed breeding and cultural traditions? Can’t we conserve our idiosyncratic identity and belong to mankind’s universal one at once leaving hatred aside and be committed to what is our undeniable responsibility?<br /><br /><i>“Few doctrines throughout History have been able to eradicate hatred, most of them have simply tried to deflect it from one object to another: to the infidel, the foreigner, the apostate, the master, the slave, the father. Naturally, hatred is only called hatred when we see it in others; the hatred which is in ourselves bears a thousand different names.”</i> <br /><br />Naive utopia? Maybe. But I do respond to Maalouf’s stubborn resistance to succumb to despondency.<br />Obvious concepts? Maybe. But I dream of a day when a human being, many years from now, might pick this volume from a dusty library and shrug his/her shoulders in amazement at how retarded civilization was in the 21stC to still have the need to write essays about such elementary concerns.<br />Idealistic route to follow? Maybe. But that doesn’t justify that we continue walking in the opposite direction, does it?<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9745436520362797823" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1718535795" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1718535795" />
    <a title="Lisa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32532774-lisa"><img alt="Lisa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1480492067p2/32532774.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1718535795?book_show_action=true">Aug 04, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lisa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lisa" href="/user/show/32532774-lisa">Lisa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780142002575" href="/book/show/212932.In_the_Name_of_Identity">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32532774-lisa?shelf=nonfiction">nonfiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32532774-lisa?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1718535795" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2815638362284415865">I return to this essay almost every year!<br/><br/>And in 2016, Maalouf's reflections on the link between identity, violence, and the desperate need to define belonging by exclusive participation in a specific group seem more urgently needed than ever. <br/><br/>While reading <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/64533.The_Crusades_Through_Arab_Eyes' title='The Crusades Through Arab Eyes by Amin Maalouf' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Crusades Through Arab Eyes</a>, another one of his masterpieces on changing perspectives to gain understanding of historical processes, I go back to "In the Name of Identity" to check my thoughts. <br/><br/>It is a universally valid account of the diffe</span>
  <span id="freeText2815638362284415865" style="display:none">I return to this essay almost every year!<br /><br />And in 2016, Maalouf's reflections on the link between identity, violence, and the desperate need to define belonging by exclusive participation in a specific group seem more urgently needed than ever. <br /><br />While reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/64533.The_Crusades_Through_Arab_Eyes" title="The Crusades Through Arab Eyes by Amin Maalouf" rel="nofollow noopener">The Crusades Through Arab Eyes</a>, another one of his masterpieces on changing perspectives to gain understanding of historical processes, I go back to "In the Name of Identity" to check my thoughts. <br /><br />It is a universally valid account of the different layers of identity we all carry with us, and tremendously helpful when trying to navigate an increasingly polarised, yet diverse world. <br /><br />Last year, I read several excerpts of the book with 13-year-old students, worrying that they would have a hard time distinguishing between personal and community identity, but the reaction was stunning: they wanted to share the different traces of various cultural backgrounds they found in themselves, and how they struggled to combine and reconcile different traditions they are exposed to, while feeling the overwhelming power of uniform media coverage, forcing them into an almost scary uniformity of mass thought. I would not have expected young adolescents to see the uniformity of social media identity so clearly. It gave me hope while also filling me with sadness, knowing that they spend a large portion of their lives dedicated to expressing belonging by adapting a suitable identity. <br /><br />The magic in Maalouf's approach is his wish to understand all angles and perspectives of a specific attitude towards life. He is not willing to judge anything without deep knowledge of the context. And he generously shares his own identity struggles, making a case for a compassionate approach to the "otherness" of others as well as within yourself. <br /><br />For anyone moving between cultures, growing up a TCK (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/121920.Third_Culture_Kids_Growing_Up_Among_Worlds" title="Third Culture Kids Growing Up Among Worlds by David C. Pollock" rel="nofollow noopener">Third Culture Kids: Growing Up Among Worlds</a>) or simply interested in the complex identities of all human beings, this is a rewarding read. Maalouf's understanding of the topic is rooted in his multilayered family history, where different religions, languages, cultural strands join together and form a multifaceted, sometimes contrasting reality, well worth exploring in order to deepen the ideas expressed in his essays and novels. In <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/26353640.Origines" title="Origines by Amin Maalouf" rel="nofollow noopener">Origines</a>, Maalouf's family background is highlighted. It shows where he gained his views and knowledge on identity, as he grew up mirroring his developing convictions in different social and cultural settings. It is a great complement when reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/212932.In_the_Name_of_Identity_Violence_and_the_Need_to_Belong" title="In the Name of Identity Violence and the Need to Belong by Amin Maalouf" rel="nofollow noopener">In the Name of Identity: Violence and the Need to Belong</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2815638362284415865" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_873336608" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/873336608" />
    <a title="Jibran" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5407952-jibran"><img alt="Jibran" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1572721231p2/5407952.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/873336608?book_show_action=true">Mar 06, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jibran" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jibran" href="/user/show/5407952-jibran">Jibran</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780142002575" href="/book/show/212932.In_the_Name_of_Identity">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5407952-jibran?shelf=history-politics">history-politics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer873336608" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer551787552094350954">A thoughtful exposition on the clash engendered by modern ideas of nationhood between a person's many identities and allegiances. He argues for a new approach that doesn't box people into exclusive, singular identity based either on nationality, or ethnicity, or language, which, according to him, is the main source of so much violence of the last century. <br/><br/>The argument is that in the age of globalisation it is no longer tenable to have homogeneous countries with single or dominant ethnicity, lang</span>
  <span id="freeText551787552094350954" style="display:none">A thoughtful exposition on the clash engendered by modern ideas of nationhood between a person's many identities and allegiances. He argues for a new approach that doesn't box people into exclusive, singular identity based either on nationality, or ethnicity, or language, which, according to him, is the main source of so much violence of the last century. <br /><br />The argument is that in the age of globalisation it is no longer tenable to have homogeneous countries with single or dominant ethnicity, language or even culture. Therefore, there is a need to accommodate a person's many different identities for the sake of tolerance and peace. Maalouf gives his own example. He's Lebanese, native Arabic speaker, a Christian and a French national. He asks if he's not French because he has Arab origins? Is he not Arab because he's a Christian? Arabic in his mother tongue yet he writes in French and drinks France's water. Who is to deny him what part of his identity to fit him 'properly' into one country? <br /><br />A very useful read and in a way an eye-opener for the Western societies that still seem to be hanging on to the pre-World War II conception of nationhood. Maalouf argues that our failure to adjust ourselves to new realities will end up in lots of unrest and bloodshed. Two decades onwards, we're seeing his prescient analysis beginning to come true in the European countries where leaders increasingly taking on the view that multiculturalism and diversity have 'failed'. If Maalouf were to hear this, he might have said, "it is not diversity that has failed; it's you who have failed your vision of the future.'</span>
  <a data-text-id="551787552094350954" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_48053242" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/48053242" />
    <a title="Sarah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1535844-sarah"><img alt="Sarah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1574635810p2/1535844.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/48053242?book_show_action=true">Mar 02, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sarah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sarah" href="/user/show/1535844-sarah">Sarah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780142002575" href="/book/show/212932.In_the_Name_of_Identity">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer48053242" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15242103001986117941">Oh, wow. I was not expecting to find this book, more than 10 years after its publication (and references to events current at the time), still so unbelievably relevant and meaningful today. But I think this is required reading for anyone today. ANYONE. When you look at the world as it is today, and all the controversy over Islam and the Middle East conflicts, it's hard to understand where it all came from, and this book breaks it down, and theorizes that it all has to do with the question of one</span>
  <span id="freeText15242103001986117941" style="display:none">Oh, wow. I was not expecting to find this book, more than 10 years after its publication (and references to events current at the time), still so unbelievably relevant and meaningful today. But I think this is required reading for anyone today. ANYONE. When you look at the world as it is today, and all the controversy over Islam and the Middle East conflicts, it's hard to understand where it all came from, and this book breaks it down, and theorizes that it all has to do with the question of one's identity used as a weapon against those who are different and perceived to be a threat. Even when they aren't! And it also offers some suggestions for how we can all accept our multiple identities and not feel threatened. I should also state that I am not generally a fan of manifesto-type books (Aimé Cesaire, Frantz Fanon, Zola's "J'accuse," etc). I get bored several pages in; I just can't take the neverending ideology and indignant declarations. But this, on the other hand, actually hooked me and drew me in and I could not agree more with everything Maalouf has to say. Highly, HIGHLY recommended!</span>
  <a data-text-id="15242103001986117941" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2086201610" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

You never can tell. Some books intended to be immortal expire immediately, while another, regarded as a schoolboy diversion, survives. But hope springs eternal.<br /><br />For this book, neither a literary work nor a diversion, I make a different wish. May my grandson, growing up and finding it one day by chance on the family bookshelves, look through the pages, read a passage or two, then put it back in the dusty corner where he found it, shrugging his shoulders<br />and marvelling that in his grandfather's day such things still needed to be said."</span>

<div id="review_480088709" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/480088709" />
    <a title="Andrea Hickman Walker" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4258114-andrea-hickman-walker"><img alt="Andrea Hickman Walker" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1537271253p2/4258114.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/480088709?book_show_action=true">Dec 17, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andrea Hickman Walker" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andrea Hickman Walker" href="/user/show/4258114-andrea-hickman-walker">Andrea Hickman Walker</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780142002575" href="/book/show/212932.In_the_Name_of_Identity">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4258114-andrea-hickman-walker?shelf=health">health</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4258114-andrea-hickman-walker?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4258114-andrea-hickman-walker?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4258114-andrea-hickman-walker?shelf=politics">politics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4258114-andrea-hickman-walker?shelf=language">language</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4258114-andrea-hickman-walker?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4258114-andrea-hickman-walker?shelf=religion">religion</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4258114-andrea-hickman-walker?shelf=psychology">psychology</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer480088709" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2298000069040204946">This is an absolutely fascinating topic, that I've never really thought much about. I am me. Yes I'm South African, female, an Aspie, arthritic, a librarian, an archaeologist, a wife, a roleplayer, and so on. But to me, I'm just me. And other people are just them (though, given the whole Aspie thing, sometimes they're just inanimate objects). I think, in fact, that it's the Aspie thing that prevents me from seeing identities as so fundamental - I cannot conceive of a person as just a Buddhist. T</span>
  <span id="freeText2298000069040204946" style="display:none">This is an absolutely fascinating topic, that I've never really thought much about. I am me. Yes I'm South African, female, an Aspie, arthritic, a librarian, an archaeologist, a wife, a roleplayer, and so on. But to me, I'm just me. And other people are just them (though, given the whole Aspie thing, sometimes they're just inanimate objects). I think, in fact, that it's the Aspie thing that prevents me from seeing identities as so fundamental - I cannot conceive of a person as just a Buddhist. They're a bunch of other things as well. Sometimes those things will conflict with each other and then that person has to weigh up their own feelings, principles and beliefs to decide how to act (or react). Apparently that's not the way the majority of people see things. Apparently many people prioritise one aspect of what makes them them - depending on what's being attacked or celebrated at the time, or on what makes them feel that they belong - and suppress the other parts. I don't know how to do that and I only have a rather limiting understanding of how other people are able to do that, or feel the need to do that. I can only imagine what it must feel like, but I don't like it at all.<br /><br />In short, I completely agree with Maalouf's argumen that we should celebrate human diversity, at the same time as celebrating all the different things about us that join us to others who share that (other librarians, other Aspies, other South Africans, in my case). Everyone should be free to be who they truly are, with all the odd little bits that make them them without feeling marginalised or excluded (or, worse in my opinion, patronised).</span>
  <a data-text-id="2298000069040204946" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3568653838" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3568653838" />
    <a title="Ahmed Fathy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/52412258-ahmed-fathy"><img alt="Ahmed Fathy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1524677129p2/52412258.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3568653838?book_show_action=true">Sep 27, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ahmed Fathy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ahmed Fathy" href="/user/show/52412258-ahmed-fathy">Ahmed Fathy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780142002575" href="/book/show/212932.In_the_Name_of_Identity">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3568653838" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17006612285509464127">“What makes me myself rather than anyone else is the very fact that I am poised between two countries, two or three languages and several cultural traditions. It is precisely this that defines my identity. Would I exist more authentically if I cut off a part of myself.”<br/><br/>Amin Maalouf shares a very interesting reflection on the deadly effects that identity can trigger when its meaning is oversimplified. Having been asked many times “Do you identify more with France or Lebanon?”, the author advocate</span>
  <span id="freeText17006612285509464127" style="display:none">“What makes me myself rather than anyone else is the very fact that I am poised between two countries, two or three languages and several cultural traditions. It is precisely this that defines my identity. Would I exist more authentically if I cut off a part of myself.”<br /><br />Amin Maalouf shares a very interesting reflection on the deadly effects that identity can trigger when its meaning is oversimplified. Having been asked many times “Do you identify more with France or Lebanon?”, the author advocates for a better understanding of identity grounded in cultural richness and plurality over the seemingly widespread phenomenon of “typecasting individuals”. In doing so, he rejects the assumption that it is necessary for us to discover or to choose one true sense of belonging, an essence determined once and for all.<br /><br /> According to Amin Maalouf, identity should not be compartmentalized: “You can’t divide it up into halves or thirds or any other separate segments”. Each and every one of us is made up of multiple identities: cultural, linguistic, religious, national, political…which can change with time without necessarily surrendering to a form of hierarchy. It is this very attempt to dissolve all the components which make us who we are which creates violent confrontations to prove the superiority of one identity over another. We are left with fractures across the political, religious, and ethnic spectrum, preventing us from celebrating diversity in all its richness and beauty. Thus, identity becomes “deadly” when its complexity is denied in favor of a separation between self and other. The rejection of plurality by some could, on the contrary, create bridges between the diverse cultures and communities shaping our world.<br /><br />I liked this book a lot because Amin Maalouf analyzes a complex and timely subject based on personal experiences of our everyday lives. The author shares examples from his own life: as a Christian whose mother tongue is Arabic, the sacred language of Islam, he seized this opportunity to connect with more people whereas some have solely focused on what they have deemed to be a paradox. The answers to his important questions demonstrate strong willpower to change the world for the better, and this very hope encourages us to reflect upon the diverse mechanisms of identity shaping our daily lives. Reading this essay has allowed me to better understand why a rejection of difference can party explain, without ever justifying, the explosion of crimes committed in the name of identity. Beyond the author’s illuminating deconstruction of the concept, what I enjoyed most is the genuine lesson of humanity and tolerance formulated, and which has never been more relevant these days.<br />Finally , Excuse my language mistakes. I'm still working on my writing skills :D</span>
  <a data-text-id="17006612285509464127" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1556132759" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1556132759" />
    <a title="ReemK10 (Paper Pills)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15726428-reemk10-paper-pills"><img alt="ReemK10 (Paper Pills)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1507553429p2/15726428.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1556132759?book_show_action=true">Feb 21, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="ReemK10 (Paper Pills)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="ReemK10 (Paper Pills)" href="/user/show/15726428-reemk10-paper-pills">ReemK10 (Paper Pills)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1556132759" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer10660150488661181754">I did enjoy reading Amin Maalouf's digression which resonated greatly with me. There are so many of us who simply don't feel at home anywhere, and that is why we need our books.We live in a world that needs to be more accepting of others and a whole lot kinder. We need to sit at dinner tables with friends from different countries around the world and share our stories.<br/>This is truly a very compelling read, and one that is very necessary.</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hugo" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hugo" href="/user/show/838781-hugo">Hugo</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780142002575" href="/book/show/212932.In_the_Name_of_Identity">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/838781-hugo?shelf=peace-studies">peace-studies</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/838781-hugo?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/838781-hugo?shelf=history">history</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer210390486" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17739665559887739412">I can't help but hope that this would become required reading for High School students around the world.<br/><br/>Maalouf, with extreme simple yet well-selected language, deconstructs well established notions of nationality and identity that prevails today - the idea that people are attached to a certain, and single, nationality or national identity. He questions it, argues against it, and eventually arrives at the conclusion that it is not only absurd, but an extreme pity that people should limit themsel</span>
  <span id="freeText17739665559887739412" style="display:none">I can't help but hope that this would become required reading for High School students around the world.<br /><br />Maalouf, with extreme simple yet well-selected language, deconstructs well established notions of nationality and identity that prevails today - the idea that people are attached to a certain, and single, nationality or national identity. He questions it, argues against it, and eventually arrives at the conclusion that it is not only absurd, but an extreme pity that people should limit themselves to one identity, when, in fact, people would be more interesting if they embraced the many elements of their identity that "really" makes them.<br /><br />As an example, he takes his own personality. Having lived half of his life in France and the other half in Lebanon, having grown up a Christian in a Muslim country - he says that it would be extremely untrue for himself to say that he was either 100% Lebanese or 100% Christian as so many other influences (the French and the Islamic) have also shaped his identity.<br /><br />In a second part of the book he also attempts to deconstruct misconceptions about the "Muslim" world. But I found the initial "identity" part of the book fascinating!</span>
  <a data-text-id="17739665559887739412" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText4523141341056031973" style="display:none">"What's happening [Covid19 pandemics] is terrifying but it is also fascinating"<br />In interview to Portuguese paper "i"*.<br /><br /><br /><br />NOTES<br /><br />ālif/ In 1976, Amin Maalouf left Lebanon for France. He read Gulliver's travels and Dumas in Arabic. He spent 22 years in France, though.<br /><br />bā̛/ About belongings, Maalouf says the situation is more complicated on the other side of the Rhine, like, for the Turks born in Germany. Etc.<br /><br />tā̛/ Every individual is a meeting ground for many alligeances. Sometimes these loyalties conflict. However, some are "ordered" or "pressed" to take sides.<br /><br />thā̛/ Author gained lessons from a writing life: "be wary of words", like "identity". He approaches cases of Muslims, Serbs, Croats, in Sarajevo. Then Hutus and Tutsis. Turks and Kurds. He concludes the hierarchy of the identity elements (language, religion, class, nationality...) is not immutable.<br /><br />jim/ Maalouf spends time examining his identity, one of a Christian Arab. <div id="review_2593112700" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2593112700" />
    <a title="Eileen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/945127-eileen"><img alt="Eileen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1465156813p2/945127.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2593112700?book_show_action=true">Mar 01, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Eileen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Eileen" href="/user/show/945127-eileen">Eileen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780142002575" href="/book/show/212932.In_the_Name_of_Identity">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/945127-eileen?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2593112700" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9602518735491835176">My rating: 3.5<br/><br/>While I do not agree with everything Maalouf says in his very thought-provoking book, I do feel he raises many interesting points about identity, particularly the migrant, or emigrant or expatriate (choose your favorite term) identity. This is a great discussion book, ideal for book groups. My local book group read this in December 2018 and we literally could not stop talking about it. <br/><br/>I particularly enjoyed Maalouf’s discussion of language and identity in Chapter 4 and this is one</span>
  <span id="freeText9602518735491835176" style="display:none">My rating: 3.5<br /><br />While I do not agree with everything Maalouf says in his very thought-provoking book, I do feel he raises many interesting points about identity, particularly the migrant, or emigrant or expatriate (choose your favorite term) identity. This is a great discussion book, ideal for book groups. My local book group read this in December 2018 and we literally could not stop talking about it. <br /><br />I particularly enjoyed Maalouf’s discussion of language and identity in Chapter 4 and this is one area where I totally agree with him. Maalouf regards “any attempt to separate language from identity as neither possible nor desirable.” He adds that, “Language is bound to remain the mainspring of cultural identity, and linguistic diversity the mainspring of all other diversities.” (p.132) Maalouf says, “It is essential that we establish clearly and without ambiguity, and that we watch over tirelessly, the right of every man to retain and to use freely the language which identifies him and with which he identifies.” This makes me think of an article I read the other day of a professor at a university in the States who reprimanded two Chinese students for speaking in Chinese in a dorm because she felt they should be speaking English at all times to improve their command of it. The general response was outrage at the professor and she was eventually obliged to resign from her administrative position. Why shouldn’t the students be free to converse in their language of identity as long as they use English in class? Maalouf’s comments also bring to mind the laudable common practice in the States these days of offering (over the phone, in the subway, in department stores) Spanish as an option in addition to English. <br /><br />I have always been a proponent of people learning other languages in addition to their language of identity. A few years ago, when my university was considering eliminating certain classes in Russian, Italian and French I wrote a formal letter of protest. (Not sure if it was in part due to my letter and others like it, but the classes were retained in the end). When we learn a new language we also open ourselves up to new literature and a whole new culture. When I was in college, each foreign language had its own department. At my alma mater (SUNY Albany), all languages are now appropriately merged into the Languages, Literatures and Cultures department. I wish that all Americans would read and take to heart Maalouf’s statement that, “It will always be a serious handicap not to know English, but it will also, more and more, be a serious handicap to know English only.” <br /><br />Maalouf believes that nowadays everybody needs three languages. He proposes a three-fold solution to our world’s problems of identity:<br />- Preserve the language of one’s own identity<br />- Make the teaching of English as a third language common everywhere<br />- Encourage young people to adopt a second language, not just European ones but also “Arabic, Chinese, Japanese and a hundred other languages more rarely studied.” I love his characterization of this second language, which would be freely chosen by the individual, as the “language of the heart, the adopted language, the language you have married, the language you love.” (p. 140)<br />Maalouf says that these three measures “constitute a wise policy for us to adopt if we want the fantastic current expansion in communications to bring us enrichment at all levels, rather than impoverishment, general mistrust and troubled minds.<br /><br />Sorry if I have rambled here, but this subject is close to my heart. And it is only one of many that Maalouf raises. He also addresses globalization, racism, religious fundamentalism, tribalism, ethnic conflicts, immigration… the list goes on and on. If you are looking for some food for thought on the plight of our modern world, take a look at this book. <br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9602518735491835176" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1746912963" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1746912963" />
    <a title="William Burr" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/30734070-william-burr"><img alt="William Burr" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1746912963?book_show_action=true">Sep 02, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="William Burr" class="user" itemprop="url" name="William Burr" href="/user/show/30734070-william-burr">William Burr</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9782253150053" href="/book/show/212934.Les_Identit_s_meurtri_res">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1746912963" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15030072566406887067">This is a charming little book, which I’m glad to have read because it injected some new ideas into my brain. That said, the second half is a little pedantic and I pushed myself to read on through. <br/><br/>Amin Maalouf sets out with a question - why does it always bother him so much, when people insist that he decide whether he feels more French or Lebanese? The native of Lebanon has lived in France for many years now, and he writes in French. For him, the urge to favorise one identity over another is a</span>
  <span id="freeText15030072566406887067" style="display:none">This is a charming little book, which I’m glad to have read because it injected some new ideas into my brain. That said, the second half is a little pedantic and I pushed myself to read on through. <br /><br />Amin Maalouf sets out with a question - why does it always bother him so much, when people insist that he decide whether he feels more French or Lebanese? The native of Lebanon has lived in France for many years now, and he writes in French. For him, the urge to favorise one identity over another is a root of conflict in the world, as is identification in general. He goes on to show that even though he is “Lebanese,” he has infinite differences with almost every single other Lebanese person - even his own father, for example. So what is it exactly that makes him Lebanese? “…grâce à chacune de mes appartenances, prise séparément, j’ai une certaine parenté avec un grand nombre de mes semblables ; grâce aux mêmes critères, pris tous ensemble, j’ai mon identité propre, qui ne se confond avec aucune autre.” <br /><br />His attack on identity reminds me a lot of Buddhist philosophy, which states that all identification is false, and leads to suffering. I feel like the same idea is leading him to dissect labels like “French” and “Lebanese.”<br /><br />Maalouf lays out several other interesting ideas, that felt equally like they were blowing apart stereotypes. For instance, while we see Christianity as more tolerant than Islam today, for centuries the opposite was true; minority sects were tolerated for ages in Arab countries, which was not at all the case in Christian Europe. Maalouf points out that Islamic society’s tolerance corresponded with its confidence and overall sense of well-being as a culture. <br /><br />He also argues against defining societies by their religion, and points out that great social change and ideas throughout history have more often than not met with resistance from religion, rather than support. He wishes to see more how countries influence religion, and not only, how religion influences a country.<br /><br />In the second half of the book he looks more at concrete solutions to the problem of an identity-conflict-ridden world, in the age globalization. He suggests forcing all children to learn English plus a third language. This part of the book felt stiff to me. <br /><br />Overall though, an interesting read. Here are some of my favourite quotes.<br /><br />“Pour aller résolument vers l’autre, il faut avoir les bras ouverts et la tête haute, et l’on ne peut avoir les bras ouverts que si l’on a la tête haute. Si, à chaque pas que l’on fait, on a le sentiment de trahir les siens, et de se renier, la démarche en direction de l’autre est viciée ; si celui dont j’étudie la langue ne respecte pas la mienne, parler sa langue cesse d’être un geste d’ouverture, il devient un acte d’allégeance et de soumission.”<br /><br />“Ce qui est sacré, dans la démocratie, ce sont les valeurs, pas les mécanismes. Ce qui doit être respecté, absolument et sans la moindre concession, c’est la dignité des êtres humains, de tous les êtres humains, femmes, hommes et enfants, quelles que soient leurs croyances ou leur couleur, et quelle que soit leur importance numérique ; le mode de scrutin doit être adapté à cette exigence.”<br /><br />“Pour ce livre , qui n’est ni un divertissement ni une oeuvre littéraire, je formulerai le voeu inverse : que mon petit-fils, devenu homme, le découvrant un jour par hasard dans la bibliothèque familiale, le feuillette, le parcoure un peu, puis le remette aussitôt à l’endroit poussiéreux d’où il l’avait retiré, en haussant les épaules, et en s’étonnant que du temps de son grand-père on eût encore besoin de dire ces choses-là.”</span>
  <a data-text-id="15030072566406887067" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

<span id="freeTextContainer18198963771783401545">Definitely made me think about the way we construct our identities, and the problems that arise when identities are constructed in conflict with other identities. The text focuses a lot on the idea of making sure groups can feel included in the globalization of the world, because if they don't, “any human community that feels humiliated or fears for its existence will tend to produce killers. And these killers will commit the most dreadful atrocities in the belief that they are right to do so an</span>
  <span id="freeText18198963771783401545" style="display:none">Definitely made me think about the way we construct our identities, and the problems that arise when identities are constructed in conflict with other identities. The text focuses a lot on the idea of making sure groups can feel included in the globalization of the world, because if they don't, “any human community that feels humiliated or fears for its existence will tend to produce killers. And these killers will commit the most dreadful atrocities in the belief that they are right to do so and deserve the admiration of their fellows in this world and bliss in the next...What we have to do is prevent the conditions occurring that will bring the monster forth” (28).<br /><br />Top quotes:<br />“People often see themselves in terms of whichever one of their allegiances is most under attack” (26)<br /><br />“We must act in such a way as to bring about a situation in which no one feels excluded from the common civilization that is coming into existence;in which everyone may be able to find the language of his own identity and some symbols of his own culture; and in which everyone can identify to some degree with what he sees emerging in the world about him, instead of seeking refuge in an idealized past” (163).<br />“In parallel to this, everyone should be able to include in what he regards as his own identity a new ingredient, one that will assume more and more importance in the course of the new century and the new millennium: the sense of belonging to the human adventure as well as his own” (164).<br /></span>

<div id="review_2173690" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2173690" />
    <a title="Perduto boy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/120067-perduto-boy"><img alt="Perduto boy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1185558053p2/120067.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2173690?book_show_action=true">Jun 20, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Perduto boy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Perduto boy" href="/user/show/120067-perduto-boy">Perduto boy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

<div id="review_1760393253" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1760393253" />
    <a title="Cyrus Carter" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7678825-cyrus-carter"><img alt="Cyrus Carter" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1329028858p2/7678825.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1760393253?book_show_action=true">Sep 17, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cyrus Carter" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cyrus Carter" href="/user/show/7678825-cyrus-carter">Cyrus Carter</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781860446729" href="/book/show/29925263-on-identity">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1760393253" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7822341301328317612">On Identity is an excellent primer on acceptance of the diversity that lies within ourselves, our family and those around us. For those of us who have grown up in the midst of cultural, gender, sexual and other diversity, the book acts as a reminder to remain mindful of it within current circumstances. For those having grown up in a more monochromatic atmosphere, the book highlights points necessary to become more enlightened. For us all, it acts to remind us that there is the other within self.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2718857467" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2718857467" />
    <a title="Khaled" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/40910071-khaled"><img alt="Khaled" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1568648198p2/40910071.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2718857467?book_show_action=true">Mar 01, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Khaled" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Khaled" href="/user/show/40910071-khaled">Khaled</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781860467295" href="/book/show/326480.On_Identity">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2718857467" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14302151571879619838">On Identity is a book that challenges the mind and the heart of its reader. You cannot but go with the flow of Maalouf’s brilliant questions that open a new horizon of an undiscovered world of identity. It dwells deep down to the original question who am I and where do I belong? Can identity be only one to each person, or can there be multiple identities that a person could identify himself or herself to? Am I Lebanese or Muslim, Christian? Am I Lebanese, French or both? Am I African, American o</span>
  <span id="freeText14302151571879619838" style="display:none">On Identity is a book that challenges the mind and the heart of its reader. You cannot but go with the flow of Maalouf’s brilliant questions that open a new horizon of an undiscovered world of identity. It dwells deep down to the original question who am I and where do I belong? Can identity be only one to each person, or can there be multiple identities that a person could identify himself or herself to? Am I Lebanese or Muslim, Christian? Am I Lebanese, French or both? Am I African, American or both? Or even how does a Yugoslavian citizen identify himself or herself before and after the 90s war? Can I leave part of my identity behind? <br />As for Language, how can a person has an identity if he is not equipped with the needed language? The most cruel theft is to steal a nation’s belonging by corrupting its language. France has succeeded in demolishing Algeria’s mother tongue replacing it with French. However, France granted not the Algiers fully fledged French citizenship. This is a tragic truth! For Maalouf, one should learn three languages, his/her mother tongue, English, and a third language that he could choose on his own. Without speaking the language, one would never be able to find his identity or his belonging to a land or a nation.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14302151571879619838" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3419155986" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3419155986" />
    <a title="Eleftherios Makedonas" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/81189728-eleftherios-makedonas"><img alt="Eleftherios Makedonas" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588092331p2/81189728.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3419155986?book_show_action=true">Jul 06, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Eleftherios Makedonas" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Eleftherios Makedonas" href="/user/show/81189728-eleftherios-makedonas">Eleftherios Makedonas</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780142002575" href="/book/show/212932.In_the_Name_of_Identity">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/81189728-eleftherios-makedonas?shelf=anti-nationalism">anti-nationalism</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3419155986" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10059808101057381429">Despite its initially promising, but finally utterly deceptive title, the book does not in fact address the ONLY relevant issue regarding any mind-projected psycholigical shelter like religion or nation and the like, i.e., that they all have been invented by man due to his/her omnipotent need to belong, in other words, to possess an identity whatsoever. <br/><br/>Having missed this point of cardinal importance already from the outset, Maaluf has no other option but to consume himself in innumerable banali</span>
  <span id="freeText10059808101057381429" style="display:none">Despite its initially promising, but finally utterly deceptive title, the book does not in fact address the ONLY relevant issue regarding any mind-projected psycholigical shelter like religion or nation and the like, i.e., that they all have been invented by man due to his/her omnipotent need to belong, in other words, to possess an identity whatsoever. <br /><br />Having missed this point of cardinal importance already from the outset, Maaluf has no other option but to consume himself in innumerable banalities and clichès about the supposed challenges our globalized era poses to everybody and the need to embrace them, in the spirit of the long-ago worn-out and obviously inadequate as it has been proven repeatedly, old set of values of the European Enlightenment: open-mindness, an effort to understand the Other, rejection of fanaticism in any of its forms, etc. <br /><br />However, one has to remind Maalouf of an equally old and almost banal truth of universal validity for the human animal: good will, musts and must-nots, moral precepts and incitements, hopes and dreams of 'human' behaviour are not in themselved enough to stop human aggression, nor to dissolve human beings' devouring need for identity! The problem is still there, even though Maalouf gas written his book!</span>
  <a data-text-id="10059808101057381429" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2383724583" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2383724583" />
    <a title="David" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7175349-david"><img alt="David" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2383724583?book_show_action=true">May 06, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/7175349-david">David</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781860467295" href="/book/show/326480.On_Identity">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2383724583" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer87460732403600529">Found this on the shelves at Acorn, the wonderful used bookstore that close its doors earlier this year. Not sure why this book insisted on coming home with me, but I'm glad it did. The author is from Lebanon, but lives in France. The book is a UK edition, but carries the stamp of the bookstore "Shakespeare and Company, Kilometer Zero Paris." First published in French in the late 1990s, this edition is from 2000, so it's not hot off the press. But it is engaging, thoughtful, and definitely well </span>
  <span id="freeText87460732403600529" style="display:none">Found this on the shelves at Acorn, the wonderful used bookstore that close its doors earlier this year. Not sure why this book insisted on coming home with me, but I'm glad it did. The author is from Lebanon, but lives in France. The book is a UK edition, but carries the stamp of the bookstore "Shakespeare and Company, Kilometer Zero Paris." First published in French in the late 1990s, this edition is from 2000, so it's not hot off the press. But it is engaging, thoughtful, and definitely well worth a read. <br /><br />Reading this book gave me a bit of hope for humanity, or at least reminds me why I try to choose Hope over Despair. I've been very sick (walking pneumonia, apparently), so a bit of uplift was very welcome.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1866799100" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1866799100" />
    <a title="Katia N" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7381993-katia-n"><img alt="Katia N" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1370960553p2/7381993.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1866799100?book_show_action=true">Jan 13, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Katia N" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Katia N" href="/user/show/7381993-katia-n">Katia N</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780142002575" href="/book/show/212932.In_the_Name_of_Identity">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1866799100" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6811537346827887401">This book has been written almost 20 years ago. In these 20 years it did not lose any urgency. Is 20 years such a short term for the civilisation or does the author touch upon the eternal issue? I do not know… But I found this little book very thought provoking . I’ve highlighted probably the half of it. So it was difficult to choose what to mention here… <br/><br/>Mr Maalouf’s language is lucid, the ideas are profound, even if some of them might seem simple. I think, this is because they are very well ar</span>
  <span id="freeText6811537346827887401" style="display:none">This book has been written almost 20 years ago. In these 20 years it did not lose any urgency. Is 20 years such a short term for the civilisation or does the author touch upon the eternal issue? I do not know… But I found this little book very thought provoking . I’ve highlighted probably the half of it. So it was difficult to choose what to mention here… <br /><br />Mr Maalouf’s language is lucid, the ideas are profound, even if some of them might seem simple. I think, this is because they are very well articulated. I admire author’s courage and persistence of working through them patiently. <br /><br />The main premise is that our identity is multidimensional. There are numerous parts of it. And only its entirety makes every human being unique. However, if any part of our identity is threatened externally (be it ethnicity, nationality, class or religion), this part becomes domineering and might lead to many irrational and ugly manifestations. With this phenomena, he explains the bloody conflicts throughout the history of 20th century and our time.<br /><br />He points out that reciprocality between different human beings and between the person and the society would help to diminish this risk. His main illustration is the relationship between an immigrant and the host nation. (He is a Lebanese adopting France as his host country for the last 40 years.) He considers immigrants as bridges between the two worlds which ideally would help to cement the relationships between different nations. But he talks about two extremes: 1) immigrant thinks he could establish his little world and impose his traditions on the local population; or 2) the host country tries to make the immigrant totally reject his own cultural heritage. In both cases the person involved would face with severe contradictions within his own identity which would lead to a crisis. <br /><br />I’ve read recently a book where the main character was a girl of a mixed race. She posed herself constantly with the question “Which tribe do I belong to?” Following Mr Maalouf’s view of the world, it would be much easier for her to embrace both white and black “tribes”- or even better - to forget about the “tribes” all together and embrace herself as she is as a whole human unique being. I know that it is easier said than done. Especially when in the developed world, the identity politics is so fashionable and gives the politicians so much power to hypnotise people with rhetorics and then divide and rule…<br /><br />Mr Maalouf reflects on the phenomena of the globalisation a lot. He thinks, the globalisation makes literally everybody in the world feel marginalised to a certain extent (sorry for a lengthy quote):<br /><br />Everyone nowadays felt himself to be living to a certain extent in a minority, in exile. This is because all communities and cultures have a sense that they are up against others stronger than they, a feeling that they can no longer keep their heritage safe. Looked at from the South and the East, it is the West that dominates. Looked at from Paris, it is America that holds sway, But if you go to the United States, then what do you see? You see minorities reflecting all the diversity in the world, all needing to assert their original allegiances. And when you have met all these minorities and been told a hundred times that power is in the hands of white males, or of Anglo-Saxon Protestants, you suddenly hear the sound of a huge explosion in Oklahoma City. And who are the people responsible? Some white male Anglo-Saxon Protestants who regard themselves as members of the most neglected and despised of minorities, and who believe that globalisation is sounding the knell of “their” America. In the eyes of everyone else Timothy Mc Veigh and his supporters belong to the same ethnic group as those who are supposed to dominate the planet. In their own eyes they are merely an endangered species, with no other weapon available to them but the most murderous terrorism.<br /><br />Mr Maalouf’s response to the complexity of the world is by trying to reconcile all complex allegiances within oneself and try not to threaten others. <br /><br />“Everyone should be able to recognise himself in the symbols and no one feel excluded in the global narrative. Neither on a world scale nor within any society should anybody feel scorned, depreciated, mocked or demonised or be ashamed of his colour religion, language, name or any other ingredient of identity.”<br /> <br /><br />And, in future “Identity would then be seen as the sum of all our allegiances and within it allegiance to human community itself would become increasingly important until one day if would become the chief allegiance though without destroying our many individual affiliations.” <br /><br />It is just a vision of course. But I find it much more palatable than any alternatives which are so trendy nowadays.<br /><br />There are a lot of other issues discussed in the book: the role of islam in the modern day’s insurgencies and terror; the language and identity; modernism etc. I would pause only on one more remark he makes:<br /><br />“It would be terrible for the country have more reverence to its past than to each future”.<br /><br />How incredibly urgent it sounds when Russia is building its ideology on the nostalgia over the country which does not exist for a quarter of the century. And the the next American president has won the election by proposing “making America great again”. <br /><br />Mr Maalouf is very profound thinker and this book deserves a broad readership so everyone could pick up something enriching her/his world view (even if he/she disagrees).</span>
  <a data-text-id="6811537346827887401" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
